Odd Lots · Bloomberg 6 episode(s) · 252 insights

Bloomberg Surveillance 6/11/2026139 min56 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
A morning markets show capturing a pivotal moment where the AI bull market collides with three stressors at once: investor pushback on accelerating hyperscaler capex (Oracle, Micron, Meta), a Middle East energy shock pushing Brent toward the mid-90s, and the first ECB rate hike since 2023 into a stagflationary backdrop. The throughline is whether capital markets will keep financing a trillion-dollar AI buildout while customers resist token prices and central banks turn hawkish on energy-driven inflation.
AI capex digestion and capital-market pushback · 9AI pricing wars, commoditization and the bifurcation thesis · 6SpaceX IPO and the equity-supply problem · 5ECB rate hike and central-bank divergence · 8Iran energy shock and the oil market's muted response · 7US stagflation-lite: inflation, the K-shaped consumer and labor · 10AI policy, government stakes and the midterm risk · 7Labor-vs-token economics and the AI-jobs debate · 4
AI capex digestion and capital-market pushback 9
Capital markets are beginning to reject AI-buildout equity raises in real time: Micron's attempt to raise $70B in equity got its stock hammered, Meta's equity-raise plan drew pushback, and Super Micro fell ~28% in one session after seeking equity capital.
data_point🗣 Jonathan Ferroconviction: highMicronMetaSuper MicroOracle
So what: Watch equity issuance reception as the leading tell for when the AI capex cycle loses its financing tailwind; failed or punished raises mark the inflection.
“MICRON YESTERDAY LOOKING TO RAISE $70 BILLION IN EQUITY, STOCK IS ABSOLUTELY HAMMERED.” ▶ 1:44
Goldman Sachs projects AI-related spending could reach roughly $1.4 trillion next year versus under $1 trillion currently, an increase the hosts argue the market cannot digest quickly.
data_point🗣 Lisa Abramowiczconviction: mediumGoldman SachsAI infrastructure
So what: The sheer scale and acceleration of capex sets up supply indigestion in both equity and credit markets; size positions for volatility, not a smooth ramp.
Oracle plans net capex of $70B next fiscal year and intends to raise another $40B in equity in 2027, and already carries ~$70B of debt in the investment-grade index, the most of any nonfinancial issuer, almost all accumulated in the past couple of years.
data_point🗣 Lisa Abramowicz / Vonnie Quinnconviction: highOracle
So what: Oracle is the most leveraged, least-capitalized hyperscaler funding AI; its bonds and equity are the canary for AI-credit obsolescence and financing-fatigue risk.
Oracle is structurally more exposed to AI capex pressure than peers because it is less well-capitalized than hyperscalers like Microsoft or Amazon, warranting only a hold rating and a $194 12-month price target.
thesis🗣 analyst note cited by Jonathan Ferroconviction: mediumOracle
So what: Differentiate AI-infrastructure names by balance-sheet strength; better-capitalized hyperscalers can outspend Oracle without diluting or levering.
“WE EXPECT CAPEX TO REMAIN AGGRESSIVE IN THE FUTURE WHICH IS MORE BURDENSOME TO ORACLE INVESTORS COMPARED TO BETTER CAPITALIZED HYPERSCALERS.” ▶ 31:46
Oracle's RPO (remaining performance obligations) increased while forward revenue guidance was left roughly unchanged, suggesting RPO growth reflects diversification away from OpenAI and that some OpenAI commitments may be pushed further out.
causal🗣 Angelo Zinoconviction: mediumOracleOpenAI
So what: RPO build with flat revenue guide is a yellow flag on timing; monitor whether AI backlog converts to recognized revenue or slips right.
The cleanest place to be in the AI cycle is semiconductors, because IPO proceeds from OpenAI, Anthropic and SpaceX will be reinvested directly into AI infrastructure, with no visible end to chip demand.
thesis🗣 Angelo Zinoconviction: highsemiconductorsNvidiaOpenAIAnthropicSpaceX
So what: Position upstream (chips) rather than in capex-burdened software/cloud names to capture the buildout while sidestepping financing risk.
“THE PLACE TO BE CONTINUES TO BE SEMIS.” ▶ 34:41
Big Tech faces a structural double bind: hyperscalers must keep spending aggressively regardless of returns while new IPO-funded entrants take business that would otherwise be theirs.
framework🗣 Angelo Zinoconviction: mediumBig Techhyperscalers
So what: Incumbent free cash flow gets consumed defensively; watch margin compression at hyperscalers as new capital floods competing capacity.
“BIG TECH DOES HAVE A BIG PROBLEM IN THE SENSE THE NEED TO CONTINUE TO AGGRESSIVELY SPEND NO MATTER THE CIRCUMSTANCE.” ▶ 36:12
Hyperscaler capex plans rose ~30% in the first half of the year, with roughly half driven by increased volume/unit economics and half by higher prices, indicating providers are climbing up a steepening supply curve.
data_point🗣 Michael Pyleconviction: highhyperscalersBlackRock
So what: Half the capex inflation is price, not capacity, so AI buildout is itself an inflationary force near-term and a margin headwind; reassess ROI assumptions.
“ABOUT HALF OF THAT IS INCREASE VOLUME OR UNIT ECONOMICS, AND ABOUT HALF OF THAT INCREASES PRICES.” ▶ 1:33:38
Corporate America's AI conversation is shifting from speed of deployment to value-for-money and cost discipline, with CFOs scrutinizing whether tools at different price points deliver measurable ROI.
thesis🗣 Michael Pyleconviction: highBlackRockenterprise AI
So what: A demand-side discipline phase favors cost-effective/commoditized models and pressures premium frontier pricing; track enterprise AI budget commentary in earnings calls.
AI pricing wars, commoditization and the bifurcation thesis 6
OpenAI is reportedly considering drastic token price cuts to win enterprise customers from Anthropic ahead of expected Anthropic cuts, a move that could erode margins at both companies.
data_point🗣 Vonnie Quinn / WSJ citedconviction: highOpenAIAnthropic
So what: A frontier-model price war signals demand is not as inelastic as bulls assumed; model the margin hit across the AI stack and any read-through to AI-credit spreads.
Price cuts are a demand warning sign: if AI demand were as strong as advertised, frontier labs would not need to cut prices to win share.
contrarian🗣 Angelo Zinoconviction: mediumOpenAIAnthropic
So what: Treat token-price cuts as a contrarian demand tell against the 'demand is infinite' narrative used to justify capex.
“IT IS A WARNING SIGN IN THE SENSE THAT IF DEMAND WAS AS STRONG AS IT WAS YOU WOULD NOT NECESSARILY NEED TO CUT PRICES.” ▶ 33:32
A Citadel note argues the AI market may bifurcate: frontier models concentrate among a couple of firms with balance sheets large enough to absorb the cost, while the rest of the market migrates to cheaper, commoditized models for basic tasks.
framework🗣 Lisa Abramowicz (citing Citadel)conviction: mediumCitadelOpenAIAnthropicAI models
So what: A barbell market structure implies winner-take-most economics at the frontier and brutal commodity pricing below; position around the two likely frontier survivors.
There is meaningful model deflation underway, evidenced by commentary from companies like Palantir, even as the macro AI disruption remains powerful.
data_point🗣 Ted Mortonsonconviction: mediumPalantirAI models
So what: Falling model costs squeeze pure-model monetization but lower the barrier to enterprise adoption, supporting downstream software/application value over time.
Portfolio managers will be forced into the new AI mega-IPOs because forward index weightings will become so large that not participating guarantees underperformance.
thesis🗣 Ted Mortonsonconviction: highOpenAIAnthropicSpaceXindex funds
So what: Forced index/benchmark buying creates a structural bid for AI IPOs regardless of valuation; expect demand to overwhelm float at listing.
“PORTFOLIO MANAGERS HAVE TO GET INTO THESE NAMES... IF THEY DON'T PLAY, THEY'RE GOING TO UNDERPERFORM.” ▶ 1:20:36
The token paradox remains operative: as AI token prices fall, usage rises, so falling prices need not destroy aggregate AI revenue.
framework🗣 Julian Emanuelconviction: mediumOpenAIAI tokens
So what: Bull case for volume offsetting price; watch whether token volume growth actually outpaces price declines to validate the elasticity assumption.
SpaceX IPO and the equity-supply problem 5
The SpaceX IPO is set to be the biggest ever with a ~$75B raise at a roughly $700-800B valuation, with reported demand far exceeding available shares and over $7B in retail orders, with 20% of the deal allocated to retail.
data_point🗣 Yahaira / Jonathan Ferroconviction: highSpaceXElon Musk
So what: Record retail and oversubscription set up a strong debut but also signal late-cycle froth; size the supply absorption challenge for the broader market.
“SET TO BE THE BIGGEST EVER WITH A $75 BILLION RAISE.” ▶ 1:17:13
Evercore frames the SpaceX IPO not as an endpoint (like an AOL-Time Warner top) but as a data point on the way to higher prices, with the true wild-eyed FOMO melt-up still ahead.
prediction🗣 Julian Emanuelconviction: highSpaceXEvercore
So what: Contrarian to the 'this marks the top' crowd; if Emanuel is right, fade selloffs and expect a sentiment blow-off phase still to come.
≈ “THE TRUE FOMO, THE WILD-EYED, BUY AT ANY COST... IS STILL POTENTIALLY AHEAD OF US.” paraphrase
A coming wave of AI mega-IPOs (SpaceX, OpenAI, Anthropic) could add up to ~$4 trillion of market cap to exchanges, ending the long-running shrinkage of public equity supply that helped power the bull market.
thesis🗣 Jonathan Ferro / Savita Subramanianconviction: mediumSpaceXOpenAIAnthropic
So what: Net new equity supply reverses a key structural tailwind (buyback-driven scarcity); a regime of dilution rather than shrinkage is a slow drag on index returns.
“THE SHRINKAGE STORY WE HAVE ENJOYED FOR PUBLIC EQUITIES... IS STARTING TO EBB RATHER THAN FLOW.” ▶ 1:43:17
Senator Elizabeth Warren asked the SEC to delay the SpaceX IPO, citing its ~$800B valuation, Musk's unchecked control, and millions of Americans being effectively forced to own the shares through passive retirement accounts.
data_point🗣 Vonnie Quinn / Jonathan Ferroconviction: mediumSpaceXElizabeth WarrenSECElon Musk
So what: Political/regulatory headline risk around the listing; passive-flow forced ownership is itself a structural demand driver worth flagging.
SpaceX is playing hardball with Wall Street, handing banks minor roles with fewer fees and bragging rights, an unusual process that prices the IPO before the roadshow.
anecdote🗣 Jonathan Ferroconviction: mediumSpaceXJPMorganinvestment banks
So what: Signals issuer pricing power in hot AI deals; banks accept thinner economics for marquee mandates, a marker of late-cycle deal conditions.
ECB rate hike and central-bank divergence 8
The ECB hiked 25bp to a 2.25% deposit rate, its first hike since 2023, driven by an energy-shock inflation impulse rather than strength, while raising its 2026 inflation forecast to 3% from 2.6% and trimming 2026 growth to 0.8% from 0.9%.
data_point🗣 Oliver Crook / Jonathan Ferroconviction: highECBChristine Lagardeeuro
So what: First major-central-bank hike into an energy shock; rising inflation + falling growth forecasts define a stagflation-lite playbook for European assets.
Markets are pricing roughly 70bp of ECB hikes for the rest of the year, implying a rate-hiking cycle (hikes priced for today, September and March) rather than a one-and-done.
data_point🗣 Lisa Abramowicz / Iain Stealeyconviction: highECB
So what: If a cycle is priced and Lagarde stays data-dependent, dovish guidance could trigger a euro and front-end rates repricing; trade the gap between pricing and guidance.
Deutsche Bank argues an ECB tightening cycle cannot go far, likely one more hike in September, because upside inflation risk is matched by downside growth risk.
prediction🗣 Lisa Abramowicz (citing Deutsche Bank)conviction: mediumECBDeutsche Bank
So what: Fade aggressive ECB hiking-cycle pricing beyond September; positions for a short, shallow cycle that may need to reverse.
Jefferies warned that if the ECB raises rates this week it will likely be cutting again by Q4, echoing the 2011 mistake of hiking into a downturn and reversing.
prediction🗣 Oliver Crook (citing Jefferies)conviction: mediumECBJefferies
So what: A policy-mistake-and-reverse scenario favors being long European duration on any hawkish overshoot.
“IF YOU RAISE RATES THIS WEEK, YOU WILL BE CUTTING BY Q4.” ▶ 1:51:46
The ECB is currently more haunted by its 2022 mistake of acting too slowly on inflation than by its 2011 premature-hike error, making it more willing to err hawkish.
framework🗣 Oliver Crookconviction: mediumECB
So what: Reaction-function insight: the ECB's recency bias toward 2022 means it will tolerate growth pain to defend credibility, supporting near-term euro and front-end yields.
“THEY ARE CURRENTLY MORE HAUNTED BY 2022... WHICH WAS ACTING TOO SLOWLY ON INFLATION.” ▶ 1:52:39
JPMorgan prefers European bonds over US Treasuries and expects ECB hikes in September and next March, while viewing US growth as the stronger story; with yields ~35bp higher, a global bond portfolio yielding ~6% can be built and carry cushions further rate increases.
thesis🗣 Iain Stealeyconviction: highJPMorganEuropean bondsUS Treasuries
So what: Higher starting yields mean fixed income can deliver positive returns even if rates rise; the carry cushion is the key difference from the 2022 bond rout.
“I COULD BUILD YOU A GLOBAL PORTFOLIO BONDS THAT YIELD YOU 6%.” ▶ 47:19
James Athey argues the ECB's single mandate framing is misread: because long-run inflation is a function of growth, the neutral rate and labor-market slack, the full economic picture must feed the inflation outlook, effectively making it a quasi-dual mandate.
framework🗣 James Atheyconviction: highECB
So what: Challenges the 'single mandate justifies hiking into weak growth' narrative; if growth weakness will lower inflation, hiking is the policy error.
The Fed is expected to stay on hold for most of the year, but the next move could be a hike (possibly not until 2027) if the economy keeps climbing, and the market will price that in advance.
prediction🗣 Iain Stealey / Ira Jerseyconviction: mediumFederal ReserveKevin Warsh
So what: A 'next move is a hike' risk for the Fed is underpriced; watch for hawkish repricing in the front end if US data stays firm.
Iran energy shock and the oil market's muted response 7
Despite a second straight day of US strikes on Iran and Trump's threat to take Kharg Island, oil barely moved, with Brent around $92-94 and WTI in the high $80s-low $90s, signaling verbal intervention has lost its market impact.
data_point🗣 Jonathan Ferro / Lisa Abramowiczconviction: highIranBrentWTIDonald Trump
So what: Headline desensitization means the next real supply disruption could move oil violently with little prior buildup; positioning for tail risk is cheap.
“VERBAL INTERVENTION ISN'T WORKING ANYMORE... PEOPLE LEARNED TO IGNORE IT.” ▶ 2:18:29
Trump threatened to take Kharg Island, through which ~90% of Iranian crude exports flow, and assume total control of Iran's oil and gas, an escalation that could sharply worsen the energy shock if executed.
data_point🗣 Tyler Kendall / Jonathan Ferroconviction: highKharg IslandIranDonald Trump
So what: A strike on Kharg would remove the bulk of Iranian export capacity; this is the under-priced catalyst for a nonlinear oil spike.
“WE WILL BE TAKING KHARG ISLAND, AND OTHER OIL INFRASTRUCTURE POINTS AND ASSUME TOTAL CONTROL OF THEIR OIL AND GAS MARKETS.” ▶ 2:03:43
Oil prices have stayed contained because the demand response has nearly matched the negative supply shock, with China's oil and product imports plummeting and barrels leaking through the Strait of Hormuz in ways the market does not fully understand.
causal🗣 James Atheyconviction: mediumChinaStrait of Hormuzoil
So what: The fragile supply-demand balance hinges on weak Chinese demand; a China reacceleration or a Kharg strike removes the buffer and unleashes upside.
Oil and gas executives have warned the White House that gasoline prices could surge and US inventories could be depleted within weeks as summer driving season peaks.
data_point🗣 Annmarie Hordern / Washington Post citedconviction: highoil inventoriesgasolineWhite House
So what: Critically low inventories into peak demand set up the conditions for a fast, nonlinear price move; watch weekly inventory data as the trigger.
Evercore identifies a persistent oil price shock as the single biggest threat to the entire bull market, with a window of roughly four months and a $4.50 gas price (or triple-digit WTI) before strains show on the economy and stocks.
framework🗣 Julian Emanuelconviction: highoilEvercoreWTIgasoline
So what: Specific tripwires ($4.50 gas / triple-digit WTI, ~4-month tolerance) give a tradable threshold for de-risking equities on the energy channel.
“FOUR MONTHS AND LITERALLY FOUR DOLLARS 50 CENTS OR TRIPLE DIGIT WTI... THE STRAINS WILL START TO SHOW ON THE ECONOMY AND LIKELY THE STOCK MARKET.” ▶ 9:54
Inventory-driven oil markets can move nonlinearly once critical levels are hit, producing a dramatic, fast spike described as 'the pandemic in reverse.'
framework🗣 Jonathan Ferroconviction: highoil inventories
So what: Mental model for why a quiet oil tape is not reassuring; the physical market clears violently at low inventory, so optionality on upside is mispriced.
“ONCE YOU GET TO CRITICAL LEVELS YOU GET THIS NONLINEAR MOVE. IT IS DRAMATIC AND FAST, LIKE THE PANDEMIC IN REVERSE.” ▶ 26:57
Carnegie's analyst assesses the post-strike Iranian regime as more hawkish, having reshuffled decision-making after the assassination of key members, and likely able to hold on through the November US midterms, prolonging the oil-price risk.
thesis🗣 Maha Yahyaconviction: mediumIranCarnegie EndowmentStrait of Hormuz
So what: A regime that can outlast US political pressure means the energy-shock risk is durable, not a quick resolution; price longer-dated geopolitical premium.
US stagflation-lite: inflation, the K-shaped consumer and labor 10
US PPI rose 1.1% in May (vs 0.7% expected), 6.5% year-over-year, the fastest pace since 2022, with final-demand goods up 1.8% (largest since the series began in 2009) and 80% of that traced to a 10.7% jump in energy prices.
data_point🗣 Michael McKeeconviction: highPPIUS inflationenergy
So what: A record goods-inflation print driven by energy complicates any Fed look-through strategy and pressures equity multiples via real yields.
Energy-driven cost pressure is spreading beyond the energy sector into plastics, industrial materials, chemicals and natural gas liquids, exactly the broadening the Fed fears most.
causal🗣 Michael McKeeconviction: highPPIFederal Reserve
So what: Broadening from energy into core goods undermines the 'transitory energy' framing and raises the bar for Fed rate cuts; watch core-goods PPI components.
“IT STARTS TO GET INTO OTHER PARTS OF THE ECONOMY.” ▶ 2:11:38
RBC argues US consumers are reaching a tipping point where their capacity to absorb price shocks is fading (real wages negative, near-record-low savings rate), possibly within three to four months, ending a six-year inflation-absorption paradigm.
thesis🗣 Frances Donaldconviction: highUS consumerRBC
So what: When the consumer can no longer absorb pass-throughs, the burden shifts to corporate margins or wages; PPI and small-business pass-through intentions become the key Q3 data.
“THE SIX-YEAR PARADIGM MAY BE ON THE EDGE OF SHIFTING.” ▶ 1:00:04
Monetary policy is less functional in a K-shaped economy where a strong top-10% consumer can withstand (or needs) higher rates while a bottom-end consumer faces structural weakness, making the Fed's philosophy more important than its next move.
framework🗣 Frances Donaldconviction: highFederal ReserveKevin WarshUS consumer
So what: The K-shape splits the rate-policy transmission; favor consumer staples over discretionary and watch low-end wage and credit data for the cyclical break.
US growth is being 'allowed to run hot' on structural guardrails (AI, big government spending, tariff/IEEPA distortions) while the rest of the developed world suffers stagflationary energy-shock dynamics, a new transatlantic divergence.
thesis🗣 Frances Donaldconviction: mediumUS economydeveloped marketsAI
So what: Divergence supports US growth assets and a relative-value tilt; the US can avoid hiking while Europe must, shaping FX and rate spreads.
ADP reports a two-speed labor market: goods-sector wages (construction, natural resources, manufacturing) are accelerating at double-digit rates versus last year while services wages are flat to declining, with firms favoring bonuses over base-pay hikes.
data_point🗣 Nela Richardsonconviction: highADPlabor marketwages
So what: Goods-side labor shortages are an emerging inflation impulse; sustained shortages force base-pay increases that broadly raise labor costs, a margin and inflation risk.
The Fed may no longer need to worry about a tight labor market the way it did in prior cycles because unemployment at ~3.75% (vs mid-3s previously) coincides with a 4% headline inflation handle, raising the question of whether the labor market is even tight enough to justify caution.
contrarian🗣 Jonathan Ferroconviction: mediumFederal Reservelabor marketunemployment
So what: A reframing that the labor market may not be the constraint flips the standard hawkish logic; watch jobless claims (came in at 229k, an upside surprise) for the slack signal.
Bank of America's bear-market checklist has gone from 4-of-10 triggers in March to 7-of-10 now, and the typical bear market begins around 70% of triggers, though abundant sideline cash provides some offsetting support.
data_point🗣 Savita Subramanianconviction: highBank of AmericaS&P 500
So what: A quantified late-cycle warning; 70% of signals tripping is historically the zone where bear markets start, but dry powder argues against an imminent crash.
“SEVEN OUT OF 10 TRIGGERED... 70% OF THESE BEAR MARKET INDICATORS GENERALLY GET TO BEFORE... YOUR AVERAGE BEAR MARKET.” ▶ 1:40:50
Long-term growth expectations are near all-time highs and higher than during the tech bubble, while high-PE stocks have massively outperformed low-PE stocks, both signs of a strong speculative bid that sets up disappointment.
contrarian🗣 Savita Subramanianconviction: highS&P 500Bank of America
So what: Expectations exceeding tech-bubble levels is a setup for downside surprise; favor low-PE/value and quality over high-multiple growth.
BofA favors inflation-protected, cash-returning plays (energy, materials, real estate, large-cap value, dividend income) in a higher-nominal-GDP, higher-inflation regime, citing the 1970s-80s playbook where large-cap value crushed it, and views cash as the worst place to be.
thesis🗣 Savita Subramanianconviction: highenergymaterialsreal estatelarge-cap valueBank of America
So what: A regime-rotation roadmap: tilt from cash and high-multiple growth toward real-asset, inflation-hedged income; power being the AI bottleneck makes energy/commodities a structural, not just geopolitical, bet.
“POWER IS THE BOTTLENECK FOR AI... THAT IS BULLISH FOR COMMODITIES WRIT LARGE.” ▶ 1:46:42
AI policy, government stakes and the midterm risk 7
Trump is publicly floating US government equity stakes in AI firms, and OpenAI's Sam Altman privately floated the idea of government stakes to the administration in early 2025, while Senator Sanders proposed a one-time 50% tax on AI companies paid in stock for a sovereign wealth fund.
data_point🗣 Tyler Kendallconviction: highDonald TrumpOpenAISam AltmanBernie Sanders
So what: Government equity/taxation of AI is moving from fringe to mainstream policy; model dilution, nationalization and tax risk for AI-exposed equities.
OpenAI's openness to giving the government equity is read as sharing the burden and entrenching itself with Washington, potentially making it 'too big to fail' and a likely beneficiary of sovereign AI initiatives.
thesis🗣 Angelo Zino / Jonathan Ferroconviction: mediumOpenAIUS government
So what: Government backstop optionality is a hidden support for OpenAI's downside but a competitive distortion for rivals; watch for explicit loss-sharing or sovereign-AI contracts.
Raymond James argues that if Democrats win the House or Senate, federal laws limiting data centers will be near the top of the agenda, with Sanders and Jeffries framing data centers as the 'Death Star' driving up energy costs.
prediction🗣 Ed Millsconviction: highdata centersRaymond JamesDemocratsHakeem Jeffries
So what: Data-center regulation is a concrete midterm policy risk for AI infrastructure and utilities; energy affordability is becoming the defining political issue.
The Trump administration's strategic goal is to make the US-based token the 'reserve token' of the world, analogous to the dollar as reserve currency, by exporting US AI globally as the top national-security priority.
framework🗣 Ed Mills (citing administration staff)conviction: mediumUS governmentAI exportsNvidia
So what: A 'reserve token' export strategy underpins government financing for AI abroad and supports US chip/model demand; track the AI export program's first announcements.
“THEY WANT THE U.S.-BASED TOKEN TO BE THE RESERVED TOKEN OF THE WORLD.” ▶ 1:11:50
Republicans on Capitol Hill (via the MATCH Act clearing committee) are pushing back on Trump's easing of chip-export restrictions to China, including the H200, objecting to merging national-security and economic lanes.
data_point🗣 Ed Millsconviction: mediumChinaNvidiaH200MATCH ActRepublicans
So what: Intra-GOP friction creates two-sided policy risk for chip exporters; legislative restrictions could re-tighten China access even as Trump loosens it.
Anthropic CEO Dario Amodei published an essay supporting government power to block deployment of new AI models that present certain risks and mandatory third-party testing, which the hosts read as a competitive move to freeze rivals like xAI and OpenAI.
contrarian🗣 Annmarie Hordern / Lisa Abramowiczconviction: mediumAnthropicDario AmodeixAIOpenAI
So what: Regulation-as-moat: incumbents inviting regulation entrench leaders and raise barriers; markets may treat AI regulation as bullish for first-movers.
“IT IS SAYING TO EVERYBODY ELSE, YOU GUYS SHOULD FREEZE, WE ARE AHEAD, STOP THE CLOCK.” ▶ 2:59
Polling shows AI is rocketing up the list of voter concerns: 53% of Americans worry the technology could cost them or someone at home a job, and 77% believe AI will raise their electricity bills.
data_point🗣 Tyler Kendallconviction: highAIUS electricitymidterm elections
So what: AI job and energy-cost anxiety is now a first-order political issue, raising the probability of meaningful regulation/taxation regardless of which party wins.
Labor-vs-token economics and the AI-jobs debate 4
A key emerging question is whether AI raises wages: if workers can argue the token cost to replicate their output exceeds their pay, it could support higher wages and productivity-driven growth rather than pure job loss.
framework🗣 Lisa Abramowicz / Frances Donaldconviction: exploratoryAIlabor marketAI tokens
So what: A novel framing where token pricing becomes a wage benchmark; if labor proves cheaper than tokens for some tasks, the disinflationary AI thesis weakens near-term.
BofA notes higher-paying jobs are being paused because of AI while lower-income wage growth is ticking up, supporting a preference for consumer staples over discretionary.
causal🗣 Savita Subramanianconviction: mediumAIlabor marketconsumer staples
So what: AI is reshaping the wage distribution from the top down; the resulting income mix favors staples and pressures discretionary spending.
“WE ARE SEEING HIGHER PAYING JOBS ON PAUSE BECAUSE OF AI.” ▶ 1:42:19
AI is productivity-enhancing and disinflationary over the medium-to-long term but is putting upward pressure on prices in the here-and-now via capex, energy and labor demand.
framework🗣 Michael Pyleconviction: highAIinflationBlackRock
So what: The near-term inflationary / long-term disinflationary split means AI complicates the Fed's path now even as it argues for lower trend inflation later.
Tech leadership is messaging out of touch with ~80% of the population, prompting letters like Anthropic's, and needs to communicate AI's benefits to the bottom 70% who fear job loss and quality-of-life disruption.
thesis🗣 Ted Mortonsonconviction: mediumAIAnthropicBig Tech
So what: A widening tech-vs-public sentiment gap fuels regulatory and political backlash risk; social license is becoming a real variable for AI valuations.
SpaceX Prices IPO Shares in Biggest Debut Ever | The Close 6/11/2026105 min52 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
SpaceX prices the largest IPO ever at $135/share for a ~$1.77T valuation (52x sales), sold take-it-or-leave-it with 30% to retail; the bull case reframes it as an AI/data-compute play (orbital data centers) carrying an Elon Musk premium, while corporate-governance critics warn of CEO-for-life control and self-dealing. A parallel macro thread: AI capex estimates being revised up toward $1.4T-$2T/yr, a private-credit loss cycle turning in middle-market direct lending, and a sharp risk-on rally on a claimed (unconfirmed) Iran deal.

SpaceX IPO mechanics & demand 9
SpaceX priced its IPO at $135/share, raising ~$75 billion for a market value near $1.77-$1.8 trillion, making it the largest IPO ever and roughly double the prior record.
data_point🗣 Romaine Bostickconviction: highSpaceXElon Musk
So what: Sets a new scale benchmark for mega-IPOs and a reference valuation (52x sales) the market must digest as it begins trading.
SpaceX set the $135 price unilaterally before the roadshow rather than via book-building, deliberately removing pricing leverage from asset managers who might have bid it up.
causal🗣 Ed Ludlowconviction: highSpaceXElon Musk
So what: A take-it-or-leave-it structure means little institutional price discovery pre-open, raising the odds of a volatile, retail-driven first print.
≈ “THE REASON THEY SAID $135 EARLY IS MUSK WANTS TO TAKE THE LEVERAGE AWAY FROM THE ASSET MANAGERS WILLING TO BID IT UP.” paraphrase
The IPO was more than four times oversubscribed overall, with retail orders exceeding $100 billion and retail allocated an unusually high 30% of the deal.
data_point🗣 Bailey Lipschultzconviction: highSpaceX
So what: Unfilled retail demand could fuel a sharp gap-up at the open; watch whether buyers settle higher or fade into the afternoon.
SpaceX management deliberately gave retail a large allocation hoping that group holds the stock long-term to dampen the short-termism and panic-selling seen with institutional flips.
thesis🗣 Ed Ludlowconviction: mediumSpaceXTeslaElon Musk
So what: The float's stickiness depends on retail conviction; a disappointed retail base could amplify downside volatility instead.
“THE RETAIL INVESTORS UNDERSTAND THE STORY THEY ARE TRYING TO SELL BETTER THAN WALL STREET DOES AND THAT IS ANALOGOUS WITH TESLA.” ▶ 14:25
BlackRock's institutional order was reported targeting roughly $5 billion of the SpaceX IPO.

SpaceX, critical minerals and the China supply-chain bind 3
SpaceX's in-space data-center plan is essentially vast solar arrays, and ~95% of the world's solar panels rely on polysilicon made in China, tying the company's core future business to Chinese supply chains.
causal🗣 David Fickling (Bloomberg Opinion columnist)conviction: highSpaceXChinapolysiliconsolar panels
So what: A US national-security-aligned company structurally dependent on Chinese inputs is a hidden geopolitical risk not in the IPO prospectus.
“95% of the world's solar panels, are based on polysilicon that's made in China.” ▶ 1:09:39
Musk's proposed 100GW/year of space solar from 2030 dwarfs today's footprint (all US solar is ~200GW total), requiring colossal raw-material volumes whose supply chains run through China, which produces 100% of the world's gallium.

China produces ~95% of the world's polysilicon for solar panels and 100% of the world's gallium (used in space-grade solar chemistry), so SpaceX's ~100 GW/year of satellite solar demand (half the entire combined US solar fleet of ~210 GW) runs through Chinese supply.

Larry McDonald argues markets are entering a 2021-22-style inflation-shock regime that triggers a 'great migration' out of crowded growth/tech and bonds into hard assets, value, energy, materials, healthcare and uranium. He frames a record IPO/insider-selling wave (SpaceX, OpenAI, Anthropic, Google) plus ~$3T of lockup overhang as a structural drain on the MAG7 and S&P, while Erik Townsend and Patrick Ceresna add that the Hormuz oil crisis is being suppressed by Trump jawboning, setting up a violent eventual oil spike.

Inflation-shock regime change (2021-22 redux) 5
Larry McDonald argues markets are repeating Q4 2021, when a 'transitory inflation' trance broke and equities fell roughly 35-40% with the NASDAQ shedding ~$7 trillion of value into 2022.
thesis🗣 Larry McDonaldconviction: highNASDAQS&P 500inflation
So what: Implies a sizeable drawdown is likely as the market re-prices sticky inflation; position defensively in tech-heavy indices.
“it's it's definitely a fourth quarter 2021 redux where everybody's kind of been in a transitory trance.” ▶ 4:27
Super-core CPI is running at 3.7% and annualizing the last three months implies ~5.2% super-core by year-end, pointing to headline/core inflation of 6-8% a year out, per McDonald.
data_point🗣 Larry McDonaldconviction: highsuper-core CPIinflation
So what: If super-core (hard to fake) keeps accelerating, the disinflation trade unwinds; favors hard assets over duration and growth.
“If you annualize the last 3 months of the super core inflation that came out, you're coming out to 5.2% by year end of super core inflation.” ▶ 27:35
McDonald frames a structural shift from a unipolar disinflation decade to a multipolar world of global conflicts, higher rates and higher inflation, forcing a 'great migration' from financial assets (bonds, stocks, tech) into hard assets and the companies that control them.
framework🗣 Larry McDonaldconviction: highbondstech stockshard assets
So what: A reusable regime lens: rebuild portfolios around hard-asset owners and value rather than paper certificates.
“that's what's really what we call in the book the great migration is coming at us.” ▶ 29:06
Triple-C (tertiary high-yield) bond yields are blowing out even while stocks are near all-time highs, a leading-indicator divergence McDonald reads as a warning on the consumer.
causal🗣 Larry McDonaldconviction: highhigh yield bondsCCC creditconsumer
So what: Watch CCC spreads as an early tell that equity highs are unsupported by credit; risk-off catalyst.

The market may be entering a regime shift where leadership rotates out of crowded growth/momentum into value, hard assets, and under-owned sectors, with healthcare the clearest beneficiary because it was sold down as capital crowded into AI, semis, and mega-cap tech.
thesis🗣 Patrick Ceresna (citing Larry McDonald)conviction: mediumLarry McDonaldhealthcareXLVAIsemiconductorsmega-cap technology
So what: Signals a potential mean-reversion trade: rotate from extended AI/semis into beaten-down defensives like healthcare ahead of a broad de-risking.
“healthcare has been aggressively sold down as investors crowded into AI, semiconductors, and mega-cap technology” ▶ 0:57
The trade of the week is long XLV (closing ~$152.85) overlaid with an August 145/165 collar: buy the Aug 145 put (~$1.90, near March/April lows) and sell the Aug 165 covered call (~$1.24) for a net hedge cost of ~66 cents.
data_point🗣 Patrick Ceresnaconviction: highXLVhealthcare
So what: A concrete, low-cost, carry-efficient structure to express bullish healthcare while capping near-term equity-market drawdown risk.
“buying the August 145 put for roughly a dollar 90 ... selling the August 165 covered call for around a dollar 24. That reduces the net hedge cost to approximately 66 cents” ▶ 1:56
The XLV collar defines a downside floor at $145 (risk ~$8.51/share through expiration) and caps upside at ~$165 (~$11.49 of potential), trading off tail risk for a bounded, asymmetric payoff over the next few months.
framework🗣 Patrick Ceresnaconviction: highXLV
So what: Quantifies the risk budget so an investor can size the position knowing max loss and max gain before expiry.

contrarian🗣 Erik Townsendconviction: mediumElon MuskSam AltmanSpaceXOpenAI

The SpaceX IPO, Fable 5, AI Capex Update & Market Check w/ Gavin Baker, Andrew Fox & Clark Tang81 min51 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
Gerstner, Baker, Fox and Tang argue the SpaceX IPO (priced ~$135/share, ~$1.77T, IPO in 2 days) is a 'must-own' bet on both space and AI, framing SpaceX as a top-4 AI hyperscaler ('Elon Web Services') almost overnight after Anthropic/Google compute deals plus the Cursor/xAI model. They contend AI inference revenue and per-gigawatt monetization are rising faster than consensus, validating ~$1.5T/yr capex by 2027, and that frontier closed models keep capturing ~90% of economic value even as open source dominates token volume.

SpaceX IPO valuation & key levers 5
SpaceX is IPO'ing at $135/share, a ~$1.77T valuation, with Goldman/WSJ models projecting ~$160B revenue in 2028 versus roughly $18B last year, implying an ~8x revenue ramp over 3-4 years.
data_point🗣 Brad Gerstnerconviction: highSpaceXGoldman SachsWall Street Journal
So what: Sets the bar: the bull case requires near-unprecedented revenue compounding, so each business line (Starlink, terrestrial AI compute, model) must independently clear high hurdles.
“we know that the IPO is $135 a share 1.77 trillion” ▶ 1:54
The two biggest swing variables for SpaceX value are how fast they bring on terrestrial data centers and whether the Cursor-trained model reaches the Pareto frontier, not orbital compute.
framework🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highSpaceXxAICursor
So what: Tells investors where to focus diligence and what data releases (e.g., Grok 4.3 + Cursor model) will move the valuation.
After the Anthropic and Google compute deals, SpaceX's valuation fell from ~100x trailing TTM revenue to roughly 39x, because they added about $29B of revenue in a single month.
data_point🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highSpaceXAnthropicGoogle
So what: The multiple compresses as deals are signed, so headline 'expensive' framing may understate how quickly contracted revenue re-rates the stock.
“it was a hundred times trailing TTM revenue. Well, after the deals they signed, I think it's 39 times.” ▶ 39:24
SpaceX is positioned as a 'must buy, must own, set it and forget it' core institutional holding as the single best bet on both the space and AI future.
thesis🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highSpaceX
So what: Frames the IPO as a long-term anchor position rather than a trade, signaling large sponsor demand at the offering.
“for most institutional investors, it's a must buy, a must own. It's set it and forget it” ▶ 0:18

Core SpaceX: launch reusability & Starlink 5
Rapid two-stage reusability of Starship is the single hardest gating condition for SpaceX's economics; SpaceX plans to attempt second-stage recovery later this year and refly it next year.
thesis🗣 Andrew Foxconviction: mediumSpaceXStarship
So what: Reusability milestones are the key technical catalysts to track; failure delays Starlink V3, direct-to-cell and orbital compute.
“rapid reusability is the main thing that we're watching for” ▶ 7:40
SpaceX is scaling from ~160-165 launches last year toward high-hundreds within several years and into the thousands within three years thereafter, implying two-to-three launches per day.
data_point🗣 Andrew Foxconviction: mediumSpaceXStarship
So what: Launch cadence is the throughput constraint enabling all downstream revenue; cadence misses cap the model.
“getting into the thousands of launches probably in the next three years thereafter” ▶ 9:34
Two-stage reusability could cut launch cost from ~$1,500/kg on Falcon toward ~$250/kg and eventually well below as cost asymptotes to fuel.
data_point🗣 Andrew Foxconviction: mediumSpaceXStarshipFalcon
So what: The cost-per-kg curve is what makes orbital compute viable; quantifies the economic unlock from reusability.
“going from $1,500 per kg on Falcon, somewhere in that range to 250” ▶ 25:27
Starlink broadband has penetrated less than 1% of global households and could scale to hundreds of millions of terminals, with connectivity revenue modeled to grow from ~$10B to ~$50B by 2028.
data_point🗣 Andrew Foxconviction: mediumStarlinkSpaceX
So what: $50B is only ~0.3% of the global telecom market, framing the connectivity ramp as conservative and achievable.
“You look at the percent of global households with Starlink, it's less than 1%.” ▶ 10:47
Starlink wins on better/faster/cheaper: lowest latency and fastest connection globally today, and likely cheapest cost per gigabyte once rapid reusability arrives.
thesis🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highStarlinkSpaceX
So what: A durable product-advantage argument for taking telecom share, supporting the direct-to-cell growth thesis.
“better, faster, cheaper has been a winning formula” ▶ 13:11

Orbital compute economics 4
Orbital compute is not required for the SpaceX IPO valuation but is a meaningful call option on top of it.
thesis🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highSpaceXStarship
So what: Investors can underwrite the deal on terrestrial economics alone, treating space as optionality rather than a required leap of faith.
“I don't think orbital compute is is necessary for the IPO valuation, but it's certainly important” ▶ 22:58
Putting a gigawatt of compute in space could cost ~$5B in launch capex (≈5 MW per Starship, ~100 metric tons) versus $20-25B terrestrially for power/shell/cooling, a ~5x reduction on half the data-center bill of materials.
data_point🗣 Andrew Foxconviction: mediumSpaceXStarship
So what: Quantifies the orbital cost advantage; if reliability holds, space compute could undercut terrestrial on the non-GPU cost stack.
“the math you get to is it's about $5 billion per gigawatt of capex to put these in space” ▶ 26:51
A terrestrial gigawatt costs ~$60B today (~$35B GPUs/silicon, ~$25B land/shell/power/cooling), with the non-GPU portion likely inflationary while space power and cooling are effectively free.
data_point🗣 Brad Gerstnerconviction: mediumSpaceX
So what: Rising terrestrial infra costs plus near-free space resources widen the orbital advantage over time, contingent on satellite reliability.
“it cost $60 billion to put a gigawatt on the ground today” ▶ 27:23
Orbital compute viability hinges on satellite/GPU reliability in space, since GPUs melt and lasers fail; as long as in-space failure rates aren't dramatically worse, 'the math maths' once Starship reusability arrives.
causal🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: mediumSpaceXStarship
So what: Identifies reliability/maintenance as the key remaining technical risk to the orbital thesis.
“as long as these satellites in space aren't failing at an a at an astronomical rate the math maths” ▶ 28:25

We may never know how smart frontier models actually are because no one runs them continuously long enough (e.g., a model thinking for a year) before the next generation ships.

Nvidia has held or even gained market share against ASICs over recent years, contradicting consensus expectations of dramatic share loss on revenue, gigawatt and unit bases.

framework🗣 Gavin Bakerconviction: highNvidiaJensen Huang

Longevity: NewLimit, epigenetic reprogramming and LEV 7
NewLimit (co-founded by Armstrong, Blake Byers, and CEO Jacob Kimmel) raised $435 million for epigenetic age-reversal and has demonstrated successful reprogramming of human cells, with its first drug candidates going into the clinic next year.

NewLimit aims to do 'half of Yamanaka' - changing a cell's age without changing its type - using an AI screening system that explores ~10 quadrillion protein combinations, ingests the largest proprietary wet-lab dataset, and funnels hits through phenotypic, functional, then primate/human assays.
framework🗣 Brian Armstrong (Coinbase CEO)conviction: highNewLimitShinya Yamanakaepigenetic reprogrammingAI

Diamandis argues there is no reliable single biomarker for aging (epigenetic clocks give different morning vs night readings), so his $101M XPRIZE Healthspan measures functional age reversal - liver function, cognition, immune, muscle - rather than clock numbers.

Their highest-conviction trade is long SOFR futures and gold; they are bearish Mag 7 hyperscalers on equity issuance and capex, and bullish the memory/AI-supplier complex the hyperscalers fund.

Solving Sol Value Accrual58 min32 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
Blockworks analysts (Carlos, Toma, Ian Answorth of Cairo Street) argue SOL captures little of the value applications generate on Solana, and dissect new SIMD proposals (SIMD-547/553 compute-unit burn, Helius disinflation acceleration) as a fix gated on the still-undated Alpenglow upgrade. They also weigh AI models (fictional 'Claude Fable'/'Mythos') raising DeFi smart-contract exploit risk, and MicroStrategy/Saylor's growing structural overhang on Bitcoin and the broader crypto market.
SOL value accrual and SIMD proposals · 14AI models and DeFi smart-contract risk · 7MicroStrategy/Saylor overhang and Bitcoin · 6Crypto market structure and altcoin decorrelation · 5
SOL value accrual and SIMD proposals 14
Solana applications have become highly profitable while SOL the asset has not benefited proportionally: the ratio of application revenue to REV is roughly 5x today versus about 1x in 2023, understating the leakage because not all REV accrues to token holders.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highSolanaSOLPump Fun
So what: Quantifies the core bear case on L1 value accrual; a mechanism that recaptures app-level value would be a structural re-rating catalyst for SOL.
“the ratio of application revenue to REB is like 5x today up from like 1x at some point 19 2023” ▶ 48:28
SIMD-547 (now formalized as SIMD-553), proposed by KB of Temporal, restores an activity-linked SOL burn via a new fee tied to requested compute units, set at 0.5 lamports per CU (up from an initial 0.1).
framework🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highSIMD-547SIMD-553TemporalKBSolanaSOL
So what: A CU-based burn is denomination-agnostic, so it captures value even as apps move fees to USDC; watch the GitHub proposal and lamport parameter as the key SOL tokenomics variable.
At current usage the SIMD-553 CU burn would destroy roughly 7,000 to 9,000 SOL per day (about $190M annualized at ~8,000/day), offsetting around 12% of daily SOL issuance but not making SOL disinflationary.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highSIMD-553SolanaSOL
So what: Sizes the actual impact: meaningful offset but not net deflationary, tempering expectations of a large mechanical bid for SOL.
“If you assume like 8,000 sole burn uh based on current estimates that that's around $190 million in burn” ▶ 0:00
The key advantage of the CU-based burn is that it scales with both network usage and network capacity, so as Solana raises block limits and lowers slot times the burn rises roughly linearly.
thesis🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highSIMD-553SolanaSOLAlpenglow
So what: Aligns Solana's scaling roadmap (more CUs, faster blocks) with SOL economic value, turning throughput growth into token sink growth.
The CU burn can only be safely implemented after the Alpenglow upgrade because doing it earlier would sharply raise validator voting costs and disadvantage smaller validators, and Alpenglow has no live timeline.
causal🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highAlpenglowSolanaSIMD-553
So what: Execution risk is the binding constraint; SOL value-accrual fixes may be 18 months out, so the thesis is theoretical until Alpenglow ships.
“the main concern for me is to actually the execution of this” ▶ 50:45
Helius has proposed doubling Solana's disinflation rate from 15% to 30% so the network reaches its ~1.5% terminal inflation in roughly 3 years instead of 6, versus current ~3.8% annualized inflation.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highHeliusSolanaSOL
So what: Faster disinflation attacks SOL value accrual from the issuance side; combined with the burn it materially changes net supply growth math for SOL holders.
Prior to SIMD-96 (Feb 2025), 50% of Solana priority fees were burned and 50% went to validators, but SIMD-96 removed the burn to curb out-of-protocol transaction-inclusion deals, leaving SOL today reliant almost entirely on issuance for staking yield.
causal🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highSIMD-96SIMD-123SolanaSOL
So what: Explains the structural origin of SOL's weak value capture; the new SIMDs are a direct attempt to reverse an unintended consequence of SIMD-96.
Despite SOL making an all-time high near $260 in January, its market cap was already nearly double the 2021 cycle, illustrating how inflation diluted price appreciation.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: mediumSolanaSOL
So what: Frames SOL's inflation drag in market-cap terms: holders needed far more capital inflow to reach the same nominal price, a key dilution lens for valuation.
“sole made an all-time high of 260 or something like in January, but the market cap was already like almost double what it was like back in 2021” ▶ 47:07
Pump Fun alone accounts for over 20% of compute-unit usage on Solana, making it the largest single source of value the network currently fails to capture.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highPump FunSolana
So what: Concentration means a CU burn would disproportionately capture value from a single app; also a risk if Pump Fun migrates fees or volume away.
“Pump Fun uh currently accounts for over 20% of compute unit usage on Solana” ▶ 47:54
Applications are increasingly denominating fees in USDC rather than SOL, with Pump Fun adding USDC bonding (implying a Circle side deal), 0x's Edge product going stablecoin-denominated, and Jito's JTX pairs likely USDC, creating a risk that SOL gets bypassed even as the chain grows.
thesis🗣 Ian Answorth (Cairo Street Research)conviction: mediumPump FunCircleUSDC0xJitoSolanaSOL
So what: Stablecoin-denominated app fees threaten SOL demand; the CU-based burn is attractive precisely because it is denomination-agnostic and sidesteps this leakage.
A CU-priced fee would trigger a reaction function where protocols optimize code to cut requested CUs, but more efficient block packing simply lets more transactions fill blocks, so price-sensitive demand and total burn largely persist.
framework🗣 Ian Answorth (Cairo Street Research)conviction: mediumSolanaSIMD-553
So what: Counters the worry that CU optimization neuters the burn; induced demand backfills blocks, supporting durable burn levels.
Evidence that crypto users are price-insensitive: Benedict Brady's research showed Axiom traders being charged ~100x the actual Solana transaction fee yet continuing to trade there, supporting Solana capturing that value rather than ceding it to apps.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: highAxiomBenedict BradySolana
So what: If demand is fee-inelastic, the network can extract more value via the burn without losing activity, strengthening the SOL accrual case.
“traders on Axiom being charged like 100 X the actual Solana transaction fee and they still trading on Axiom” ▶ 55:25
Brandon Watt of Anza broadly supported SIMD-553 on GitHub but flagged that 0.5 lamports/CU may be too aggressive a starting parameter, suggesting 0.1-0.2 to avoid harming high-frequency traders and market makers.
data_point🗣 Carlos (Blockworks Research)conviction: mediumBrandon WattAnzaSIMD-553Solana
So what: Core-team support signals the proposal is viable; the lamport parameter is the live negotiation variable that determines how much SOL actually burns.
Networks cannot easily extract Apple-style app-store rents on open-source chains because apps can migrate to other chains and abstract it from users, so value capture must be protocol-native (like the CU burn) rather than a middleman fee.
framework🗣 Danny (host) / Ian Answorthconviction: mediumSolanaApple
So what: Explains why CU-based burns, not app taxes, are the realistic L1 monetization path; relevant to valuing any L1 token's long-run cash-flow potential.
AI models and DeFi smart-contract risk 7
Anthropic's newly released 'Claude Fable' is a restricted version of the unreleased 'Mythos' model that refuses certain tasks (e.g. developing frontier models, cybersecurity work) and silently downgrades to a prior model on flagged requests.
data_point🗣 Danny (host)conviction: mediumAnthropicClaude FableMythos
So what: Capability gating to whitelisted enterprises limits but does not eliminate exploit-tooling proliferation risk for open-source crypto code.
Newer AI models are markedly better at one-shotting novel tasks including auditing and hardening smart-contract code, with crypto developers and Toly (via a 'percolator' side project) reporting Fable found and fixed bugs prior models missed.
thesis🗣 Danny (host)conviction: mediumClaude FableAnthropicSolanaToly
So what: AI-assisted auditing should become standard practice; protocols that fail to continuously re-audit against new models face rising exploit risk.
AI is being used to find real exploits in crypto, with the Zcash bug attributed to 'Opus 48' and stale low-usage protocols like Ambient Finance recently hacked, with attackers concentrating on dormant capital especially on Ethereum.
causal🗣 Carlos / Dannyconviction: mediumZcashAmbient FinanceEthereumOpus 48
So what: Stale TVL on older chains is a growing attack target; capital should rotate toward actively maintained, battle-tested protocols.
“even that Zcash bug that was found was like Opus 48, right?” ▶ 12:00
Heightened AI exploit capability will widen dispersion in DeFi, concentrating deposit flows into a few conservative, never-exploited, well-resourced 'A-tier' protocols and reinforcing the existing lending-market winner-take-most dynamic.
prediction🗣 Ian Answorth (Cairo Street Research)conviction: highDeFilending markets
So what: Trade the flight to quality: blue-chip lending/DeFi names should outperform long-tail protocols as security premia rise.
Per Tarun on The Chopping Block, attacker-vs-defender dynamics have a tipping point where each exploit on a random contract hardens the rest, so the industry emerges more resilient after a painful adjustment.
framework🗣 Toma (Blockworks advisory)conviction: mediumThe Chopping BlockTarun
So what: Frames near-term hack risk as a transient cost preceding greater systemic resilience; relevant to timing DeFi exposure through the volatility.
“every like attack on a random smart contract hardens the rest” ▶ 14:46
Wallets (e.g. Phantom) are framed as the highest-impact attack surface because they are the gateway to many protocols, making a wallet-level compromise far worse than hacking an obscure DeFi protocol.
thesis🗣 Ian Answorth (Cairo Street Research)conviction: lowPhantomDeFi
So what: Wallet security is the systemic single point of failure; infrastructure-level wallet risk deserves more weight than individual protocol risk.
Much DeFi yield has historically been fake yield from untracked token emissions subsidizing returns, and the market is shifting toward real yield from genuine risk-taking and real-asset issuers (e.g. Athena/Ethena) and large asset managers launching products directly on chain rather than via existing DeFi protocols.
thesis🗣 Ian Answorth (Cairo Street Research)conviction: mediumDeFiEthenaRWAs
So what: Distinguish emissions-driven from cash-flow yield when valuing protocols; real-yield issuers bypassing DeFi as a distribution channel pressures incumbent protocol economics.

Brian Singerman: "If SpaceX Didn't Work, Founders Fund Wouldn't Exist"51 min31 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
Founders Fund partner Brian Singerman explains the firm's high-conviction, concentration-over-diversification venture model (98% bet on the founder, no reserves, beat the market only by piling capital into rare A+ companies), credits SpaceX as the existential bet that made the firm, and lays out his current pivot to backing emerging GPs. He also flags asset-seizure/wealth-tax risk driving capital out of California and a bullish, contrarian thesis on N-of-one human cultural artifacts in an AI-dominated world.
Founders Fund concentration model · 5Founder-first underwriting · 4SpaceX, Elon and the firm's origin bet · 5Portfolio deals and sizing · 4Pivot to backing emerging GPs · 3Wealth tax, asset seizure and capital flight · 4AI optimism and N-of-one human artifacts · 3Talent culture and contrarian conviction · 3
Founders Fund concentration model 5
Singerman defines venture capital narrowly as putting the most money possible into the best companies possible at the best price possible, explicitly rejecting portfolio management, reserve calculations, and ownership-percentage targeting as 'nonsense.'
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fundventure capital
So what: An LP screen: managers who optimize ownership/reserves rather than maximizing dollars into rare winners are, by this framework, running a different and weaker game.
≈ “Put the most money into the best companies possible at the best price possible. All the other stuff is just nonsense.” paraphrase
Singerman argues a venture fund that only 3x's over 10-12 years has effectively lost its investors money because that return likely underperforms a Vanguard no-load S&P index fund.
data_point🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highVanguardS&P 500venture capital
So what: A blunt benchmark for LPs: a 3x DPI over a decade-plus is a fail, not a win; reset the bar for evaluating GP track records.
≈ “if a venture fund like 3xs over the course of like 10 to 12 years, I don't even think that that beats the S&P.” paraphrase
Singerman says the only way to beat the market in venture is to deploy maximum capital into a company you can truly see is great, because legal inside access to private companies is the structural edge that public markets deny you.
thesis🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highventure capitalpublic markets
So what: Frames private-market alpha as an information-access edge; implies diversified 'spray and pray' venture cannot beat the market because it dilutes the only real advantage.
“Venture capital legal inside information... the way to beat the market is to put a ton of money into that company like put every penny that you can.” ▶ 22:48
Founders Fund does not hold reserves and instead simply raises a new fund rather than reserving capital for follow-ons within an existing fund.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fund
So what: Explains how a concentration-first firm sidesteps the reserve-discipline that caps most funds' single-name exposure.
Singerman states that he refuses to invest in public companies because he wants zero inside information and zero liabilities on anything public, reserving information advantage strictly for private deals.
thesis🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highpublic marketsprivate markets
So what: A deliberate legal/edge boundary; signals private markets remain his exclusive hunting ground even post-Founders-Fund.
≈ “I want no inside information about anything public. I want no liabilities on anything but private companies do what you know absolutely.” paraphrase
Founder-first underwriting 4
Singerman says 98%+ of his investment decision was based on the strength of the founder rather than financial diligence, and claims he has never read a financial report and does not know how to.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fund
So what: Defines the Founders Fund underwriting weighting; a reminder that the firm's hits were judgment-on-people bets, not spreadsheet-driven.
“98% plus of my decision was based on the strength of the founder.” ▶ 0:09
Singerman looks specifically for the rare 'A+ founder' (not A-minus) who is the best in the world at something novel and can leverage that into one of the world's greatest companies, and says such founders are very few.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fund
So what: The bar that justifies extreme concentration: only a handful of founders per cohort qualify, so position sizing should be binary and large.
“an actual A+ founder, of which I think there's very few.” ▶ 32:05
Singerman's personal style is to back founders later than seed, pairing the A+ talent read with observed execution, because he admits he cannot evaluate execution himself having never run a company.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fund
So what: Differentiates his sub-style within Founders Fund: he needs proof of execution, implying a later-stage entry point than pure seed investors.
Singerman cites a 'BS meter' for detecting when a founder or GP is saying what they think the investor wants to hear versus expressing a genuine natural strength, and treats authenticity as a core positive signal.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fund
So what: A diligence heuristic: managers mimicking the concentration/vertical-integration playbook to please him are a tell, not a fit.
“is somebody saying something because you you can tell they think you want to hear it... Or is somebody truly being themselves?” ▶ 36:03
SpaceX, Elon and the firm's origin bet 5
Singerman states flatly that if SpaceX had not worked, Founders Fund would not exist, because the firm essentially bet its careers on SpaceX during the 2008 period when SpaceX had three failed launches.
anecdote🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highSpaceXFounders FundElon Musk
So what: Underscores that Founders Fund's franchise rests on a single existential bet; a lens for understanding the firm's risk culture.
“If SpaceX didn't work, founders fund would not exist. We bet our careers essentially on SpaceX.” ▶ 0:00
Singerman argues Elon Musk is uniquely able to be both CEO and CTO of a company like SpaceX, a combination of novel business vision, execution, and technical depth he says has never existed before in business history.
thesis🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highElon MuskSpaceXMax Levchin
So what: Frames Musk as a non-replicable, N-of-one founder, supporting outsized position sizing in his companies.
“Elon is just there's no other person like Elon because he's the only person that truly could be the CEO and CTO of a company like SpaceX.” ▶ 26:10
Singerman says the SpaceX team and insiders knew early that Starlink, not the launch business, would be the real business, and that the launch business exists to enable downstream applications like global telecom.
thesis🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highSpaceXStarlinktelecom

≈ “we all knew that Starlink was the the business for a long time” paraphrase
Singerman notes a recent Blue Origin attempt 'just did not work,' arguing SpaceX remains an N-of-one with no other rocket company achieving comparable launch results.
data_point🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highBlue OriginSpaceX

Singerman is unsurprised by SpaceX's accelerating progress, attributing it to a snowball effect of a mission-driven (not money-driven) founder attracting and retaining the best team in the world.
causal🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highSpaceXElon Musk

Pivot to backing emerging GPs 3
Singerman is no longer doing direct investing and now allocates to emerging GPs, applying the same A+ talent test he used on founders but without imposter syndrome since he was himself a top GP.
thesis🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fundventure capital
So what: Signals a notable career shift to fund-of-funds-style allocation; LPs and emerging managers should note a new capital source with a specific selection bar.
Singerman's core test for backing a new GP is whether that manager, playing their own game, could plausibly have beaten him in his prime; a 'maybe' is enough to warrant deeper engagement.
framework🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highventure capital
So what: A concrete, high selectivity GP-selection heuristic emerging managers can self-assess against when pitching him.
“is there some chance this person could have beaten me in my prime” ▶ 33:56
Singerman observes that many new GPs are now trying to copy the Founders Fund concentration and vertical-integration strategy, which he treats as a negative signal when it is mimicry rather than the manager's genuine natural strength.
contrarian🗣 Brian Singermanconviction: highFounders Fundventure capital
So what: Cautions that the concentration playbook is now crowded and copied; authenticity to one's own edge matters more than adopting the fashionable strategy.

80,000 Hours Podcast 1 episode(s) · 31 insights

The shockingly small workforce standing between us and AI catastrophe90 min31 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
An 80,000 Hours essay (read by author Zershaaneh Qureshi) arguing AI is humanity's most pressing problem because it could replace human labour in the most economically valuable fields, trigger a recursive intelligence explosion compressing a century of progress into a decade, and create existential risks. The standout signal for an analyst: only ~1,000-few thousand people work full-time on AI existential risk versus 8,000+ at the WHO, framing AI safety as a structurally neglected, high-leverage field.
AI as a general labour-replacing technology · 4Concrete capability benchmarks and proof points · 7Intelligence explosion and economic transformation · 4Existential and concentration-of-power risks · 4Neglectedness: the small AI-safety workforce · 3Expert opinion, surveys and forecasts · 6Near-term harms and institutional lag · 3
AI as a general labour-replacing technology 4
AI is qualitatively different from prior overhyped technologies (crypto, NFTs, IoT, fusion, quantum) because it can replace flexible human labour itself, the main historical driver of progress, rather than merely amplifying it.
thesis🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAIcryptocurrencyNFTsInternet of Thingsfusionquantum computing
So what: Frames the AI investment thesis as labour substitution rather than a tool story, implying the addressable market is the entire wage bill of cognitive work, not a software niche.
≈ “the idea behind artificial intelligence is that it'll be able to do almost any work humans can do, and do so mostly autonomously.” paraphrase
AGI is defined here as AI that can compete with humans on almost all cognitive tasks, or at least the most economically valuable ones (scientific research, designing technologies, running businesses, consulting), and leading AI companies are funneling billions of dollars into being first to build it.
framework🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAGIAI companies
So what: Capital is concentrated on a winner-take-most race for the most economically valuable AI capabilities, signaling intense competitive spend and limited safety incentives among frontier labs.
The author estimates a surprisingly plausible but not guaranteed chance that AGI competing with humans on most cognitive tasks arrives within the next decade.
prediction🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumAGI
So what: A sub-decade AGI timeline is the base case to stress-test for any long-horizon allocation in compute, energy, or labour-exposed sectors.
An AI that could replace a single human engineer could plausibly be run in anywhere from thousands to hundreds of millions of copies simultaneously given enough hardware, with copy counts growing as algorithms get more efficient and compute scales.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumAIcomputesemiconductors
So what: The copyability of AI labour means an AI workforce could reach a significant fraction of the global working-age population, making compute and chips the binding constraint to watch.
Concrete capability benchmarks and proof points 7
On the GPQA PhD-level science benchmark, frontier AI went from barely better than random guessing in mid-2023 to outperforming human experts (sometimes by a large margin) since early 2025.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highGPQA benchmarkfrontier AIlanguage models
So what: A ~18-month jump from random to superhuman on expert science questions is a concrete capability-acceleration data point for sizing automation of R&D-heavy sectors.
A senior Google engineer reported Claude Code took one hour to prototype a system her team had spent a year exploring, and Anthropic built its Cowork product in under two weeks by getting Claude Code to write most of the code.
anecdote🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAnthropicClaude CodeCoworkGoogle
So what: Specific evidence of large internal productivity multipliers in software, the first sector where AI labour substitution is materially compressing development timelines.
≈ “Claude Code took one hour to generate a prototype of a system her team had spent a year exploring approaches to building.” paraphrase
Multiple AI models have reportedly achieved gold-medal performance at the International Mathematical Olympiad, and OpenAI's o4-mini solved a PhD-level problem (designed by 30 top mathematicians to be AI-proof) in about 10 minutes.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highOpenAIo4-miniInternational Mathematical Olympiad
So what: Demonstrates AI cracking adversarially-designed hard reasoning, undercutting the 'AI can't truly reason' bear case used to discount frontier-lab valuations.
Boston Dynamics is enhancing its Atlas robots with Google DeepMind AI and will deploy them for industrial work at Hyundai factories, exemplifying the convergence of frontier AI with robotics.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highBoston DynamicsAtlasGoogle DeepMindHyundairobotics
So what: Marks AI moving from cognitive to physical labour via concrete industrial deployment, a watch-item for manufacturing automation and Hyundai/robotics supply chains.
Google DeepMind's AlphaFold 3 can predict how proteins interact with DNA, RNA, and other structures at the molecular level, extending a Nobel Prize-winning system.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highGoogle DeepMindAlphaFold 3biotechnology
So what: AI-driven molecular prediction is a tangible accelerant for drug discovery and biotech R&D pipelines.
There is evidence AI systems can outperform humans on AI R&D tasks when limited to a two-hour time window, while still failing reliably on most tasks that take days or longer.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumAI R&DAI
So what: The time-horizon frontier (short tasks mastered, long tasks not yet) is the key metric for forecasting when recursive self-improvement begins.
Agentic AIs like Claude Code and OpenAI's Codex can now use computer tools, execute code, search the web, and chain multiple steps to complete extended real-world tasks with far less human involvement than text-only predecessors.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highClaude CodeOpenAICodexagentic AI
So what: The shift from text generation to autonomous tool-using agents is the mechanism by which AI begins replacing whole workflows rather than assisting tasks.
Intelligence explosion and economic transformation 4
Once AI can automate most AI R&D itself, a recursive feedback loop (better AI builds better AI) could trigger an intelligence explosion of rapid, compounding capability gains over a very short period.
causal🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumAI R&Dintelligence explosion
So what: The recursive-automation-of-AI-research milestone is the single highest-impact inflection point to monitor; it is where progress curves could go vertical.
Some researchers argue AI automation could compress a century's worth of progress into a decade, even after accounting for bottlenecks in compute, energy, and high-quality data.
prediction🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumAI automationcomputeenergydata
So what: A 10x compression of progress reframes long-dated discount rates and identifies compute/energy/data as the scarce inputs that capture the value.
≈ “AI automation could compress a century worth of progress into a decade.” paraphrase
Explosive GDP growth from an AI idea-influx is plausible but not assured; even without it, a radical qualitative transformation of society could still occur because of bottlenecks turning innovation into measured GDP.
thesis🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: mediumGDPeconomic growthAI
So what: Cautions against assuming AI capability gains translate cleanly into measurable GDP, a nuance for macro and equity-index expectations.
AI workers hold structural advantages over humans: instant copyability at scale, working faster (compressing days of processing into minutes), cheaper inter-agent coordination, and rapid fine-tuned specialization.
framework🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAI workerslabour
So what: These advantages mean the productivity ceiling of an AI workforce far exceeds human labour even before super-human intelligence, supporting aggressive automation capex.
Existential and concentration-of-power risks 4
Widespread economic deployment of AI is itself the backdrop that makes catastrophic risks more likely and harder to manage, because AI embedded in crucial economic roles is better positioned to mobilize resources to disempower humans.
causal🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAIexistential risk
So what: Links commercial AI adoption (the bullish growth story) directly to systemic tail risk, a second-order consideration for systemic-risk framing.
If elite groups control powerful AI they become far less reliant on humans, enabling unprecedented concentration of economic and political power or even seizure of power, without incentive to represent the broader population.
thesis🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAIconcentration of power
So what: Power-concentration risk implies regulatory, antitrust, and governance interventions around frontier AI control are a live political variable.
Highly capable AI could lower the bar for malicious actors to design or obtain bioweapons faster than defenses can be built, making misuse risk severe even from narrowly capable systems.
causal🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAIbioweaponsbiosecurity
So what: Bio-misuse is a risk vector independent of AGI timelines, supporting demand for biosecurity and AI-safety controls regardless of capability ceilings.
The author argues it is worth preparing for rapid, dramatic AI transformation even if the probability is only around 10%, on expected-value grounds given existential stakes.
framework🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highexpected valueexistential risk
So what: An explicit expected-value framework for acting on low-probability, high-impact AI scenarios, useful for tail-risk hedging logic.
Neglectedness: the small AI-safety workforce 3
A 2025 analysis put the total number of people working on existential risks from AI at just over 1,000, and the author estimates only a few thousand focus on the most important AGI risks.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAI safetyexistential risk
So what: Quantifies the field's extreme neglectedness, the central 'shockingly small workforce' signal and a talent-supply gap relevant to AI-safety startups and funders.
AI existential-risk staffing is dwarfed by comparison fields: The Nature Conservancy alone employs 3,000-4,000 people and the World Health Organization over 8,000.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highThe Nature ConservancyWorld Health OrganizationAI safety
So what: The benchmark comparison frames AI safety as structurally under-resourced relative to other global-risk causes, implying high marginal impact per additional worker or dollar.
AI companies face commercial incentives to develop quickly without fully accounting for societal risk, and political leaders are constrained by election cycles, so safety work is left to mission-driven outsiders.
causal🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAI companiesregulators
So what: Misaligned incentives among labs and governments suggest safety progress depends on external pressure, a structural reason regulation may lag capability.
Expert opinion, surveys and forecasts 6
Over 1,000 AI scientists and leaders, including Geoffrey Hinton, Yoshua Bengio, Sam Altman, and Demis Hassabis, signed the Center for AI Safety statement that mitigating AI extinction risk should be a global priority alongside pandemics and nuclear war.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highGeoffrey HintonYoshua BengioSam AltmanDemis HassabisCenter for AI SafetyOpenAIGoogle DeepMind
So what: Establishes that leaders of the very labs commercializing AI publicly endorse extinction-level risk, a credibility anchor for the safety thesis.
≈ “mitigating the risk of extinction from AI should be a global priority alongside pandemics and nuclear war.” paraphrase
In Katja Grace's AI Impacts survey of nearly 3,000 AI researchers, the median respondent assigned at least a 5% probability that advanced AI could cause human extinction, and a third to half put it at 10% or higher.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highKatja GraceAI ImpactsAI researchers
So what: Non-trivial median extinction odds from practitioners themselves quantify a tail risk most market models ignore entirely.
In the Forecasting Research Institute's 2022 Existential Risk Persuasion Tournament, AI domain experts estimated a 3% chance of AI-caused human extinction by 2100 versus 0.38% for superforecasters, but both groups put ~90% odds on powerful AI being developed by 2100.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highForecasting Research InstituteExistential Risk Persuasion Tournamentsuperforecasters
So what: Reveals a large expert-vs-forecaster split on risk magnitude while showing near-consensus that powerful AI arrives this century, useful for calibrating priors.
Prominent scientists including Meta's Yann LeCun (who called extinction worries 'preposterous'), Gary Marcus, Andrew Ng, and Melanie Mitchell are skeptical that AI poses transformative or existential risk, making this a contested question rather than scientific consensus.
contrarian🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highYann LeCunMetaGary MarcusAndrew NgMelanie Mitchell
So what: Flags credible dissent and the lack of consensus, a reminder that the AI-risk thesis is genuinely disputed and timelines/severity are far from settled.
≈ “preposterous” paraphrase ▶ 55:41
