Near-term harms and institutional lag 3
Current AI already causes concrete harms: chatbots generating sexualised images of children and engaging minors sexually, embedded training-data bias producing racist/sexist outputs, uncompensated use of copyrighted material threatening creators, and deepfake-enabled fraud authorizing fraudulent transfers.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highAI chatbotsdeepfakescopyrightfraud
So what: Present-day liability, IP, and child-safety exposures are active regulatory and litigation risks for AI deployers right now, independent of AGI.
Institutions respond too slowly to novel risks, evidenced by ~50 years from initial climate-change warnings to the Paris Climate Agreement and slow early COVID-19 responses, implying society may be unable to keep pace if AI capability accelerates.
causal🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highParis Climate AgreementCOVID-19institutions
So what: Institutional lag is the key reason fast AI timelines are dangerous; it suggests regulation will trail capability and reactive policy shocks are likely.
Governments are already treating frontier AI as catastrophic and national-security relevant: 28 countries (including US and China) signed the Bletchley Declaration, Biden's Oct 2023 executive order forced safety-test sharing, and the Trump administration treats AI as a national-security threat despite skepticism of catastrophic risk.
data_point🗣 Zershaaneh Qureshi (80,000 Hours)conviction: highBletchley DeclarationUnited StatesChinaJoe BidenDonald Trump

The Prof G Show · Scott Galloway 1 episode(s) · 30 insights

Anne Applebaum and Fiona Hill: Are the Autocrats Winning?62 min30 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
Anne Applebaum and Fiona Hill argue that drone-driven asymmetric warfare has rewritten the rules of conflict: Ukraine has flipped from defense to offense via a decentralized, society-led drone industry (millions of drones, a transparent 20-mile front, long-range strikes on every central-Russian refinery), positioning it as a future hub for military innovation, while a top-down Russia and a theocratic Iran face structural weakness. They contend the U.S. is squandering its core source of power (alliances and soft power / the post-1945 'operating system') in a war of choice in Iran, and that the share of the world economy operating under U.S. influence has fallen from roughly two-thirds to one-third.

Ukraine drone revolution and reordered warfare 5
Ukraine has shifted from defense to offense by remaking how the war is fought, turning its ~20-mile-wide front line into a fully transparent kill zone monitored by millions of reconnaissance and strike drones, halting Russian forward movement entirely in recent months.
data_point🗣 Anne Applebaumconviction: highUkraineRussiadronesdefense technology

“It's a 20-m wide zone which is now completely transparent and the Ukrainians can see everything in it because of their extraordinary the literally millions of drones that they've built in the last couple of years.” ▶ 28:50
Ukraine's long-range drones (described as the size of a long dining table, not toy quadcopters) have struck and damaged essentially every refinery in central Russia, most more than once, including a St. Petersburg refinery hit on the day Putin opened a major economic conference there.
data_point🗣 Anne Applebaumconviction: highUkraineRussiaPutinoil refineriesSt. Petersburg

Asymmetry and the obsolescence of big-platform defense spending 3
A $1.4 trillion U.S. military budget with expensive, sophisticated platforms may be effectively obsolete against cheap asymmetric tools, implying defense expenditure and doctrine need to be torn up rather than incrementally adjusted.
thesis🗣 Scott Gallowayconviction: highUnited Statesdefense budgetdronesdefense technology
So what: A structural bear case for legacy big-platform defense primes and a bull case for low-cost attritable systems and counter-drone tech.
“I wonder if a $ 1.4 trillion military budget with expensive platforms has just been rendered obsolete.”
Top-down, trillion-plus defense budgets are the wrong model; the World War II-era partnership of state, expert federal institutions, universities and private innovators is the path forward, but today's innovators (Palantir, SpaceX/Starlink figures, Musk, Karp) have themselves become autocrats stifling broader innovation.
contrarian🗣 Fiona Hillconviction: highPalantirSpaceXStarlinkElon MuskAlex Karpdefense budgetUnited States

Strong society is beating strong state in Ukraine: a relatively weak Ukrainian state forced civilian mobilization and flattened hierarchies that produced the battlefield innovation, whereas Putin fears mobilizing his own society and keeps everything vertical.
framework🗣 Fiona Hillconviction: highUkraineRussiaPutin
So what: A reusable lens for assessing conflict outcomes and which states can out-innovate: distributed mobilization over centralized control.
“strong society is beating out strong state and there's a lesson in that.” ▶ 34:16
Glass jaw: existential wars vs. wars of choice 3
Combatants fighting existential wars (Iran's regime, Ukraine, Russia's willingness to lose ~1,000 men/day) will absorb losses that publics in choice-wars will not, structurally disadvantaging democracies that withdraw after small casualties.
framework🗣 Anne Applebaumconviction: highUnited StatesIranUkraineRussia
So what: Predicts U.S. resolve will be brittle in any non-existential conflict, lowering the credibility of U.S. deterrence and raising risk premia in regions reliant on U.S. security.
“there's a big difference between wars that are exist existential and wars of choice.” ▶ 20:39
The U.S. has no coherent rationale for fighting Iran and is negotiating from a position with no leverage, with Trump acting as both buyer and seller and broadcasting his hand in real time, removing any Iranian incentive to make a deal.
causal🗣 Fiona Hillconviction: highUnited StatesIranIRGCTrump
So what: Implies a prolonged, unresolved Iran standoff; reduces odds of a near-term sanctions-relief deal that would re-open Iranian oil supply.
“Trump also thinks that he's the seller, not just the buyer and that is the problem because he's always telling the Iranians what he would do and in real time he's laying out everything” ▶ 5:10
Pre-war surveys consistently understate willingness to fight; Ukrainians polled as unready before February 2022 (and even doubted an invasion was coming despite CIA warnings), so similar surveys showing Europeans won't fight Russia are unreliable predictors.
contrarian🗣 Anne Applebaumconviction: mediumUkraineEuropeRussiaCIABill Burns

European and other sanctions on Russian oil have constrained Russian revenues despite higher oil prices, while imported weapons components from China remain costly, limiting Putin's ability to out-resource Ukraine.

Iran: theocracy, weakness, and the Hormuz chokepoint 4
Iran's regime structure (theocracy atop the IRGC, military and economically-penetrating groups, with the population separate) is far less hierarchical than outsiders assume, so U.S. top-down decapitation and backing exiles like Reza Pahlavi will gain no traction.
framework🗣 Fiona Hillconviction: highIranIRGCReza PahlaviUnited States

Iran may emerge inadvertently stronger by discovering leverage more powerful than nuclear material in its control of the Strait of Hormuz, which it has no intention of giving up.
contrarian🗣 Scott Gallowayconviction: mediumIranStrait of Hormuzoil
So what: Hormuz chokepoint risk is a persistent, structural tail risk to oil and shipping; a non-nuclear lever that keeps an Iran-driven oil-price premium in play.
“they've discovered something inadvertently or strategically more powerful than nuclear material and that is the straight of Hormuz.” ▶ 47:22
Both speakers reject the 'Iran as winner' framing, arguing Iran built no new industry, remains unstable, poor and unhappy, and is being left in a perilous, powerless state that is dysfunctional for a critical region historically central to the regional balance of power.

The religious/theocratic character of Iran's regime gives it a fanatical edge (belief in divinely justified, eternal legitimacy that motivates suicide missions and proxy expansion) that secular autocracies like Russia and China lack.

U.S. global power derived primarily from values-based alliances, voluntary institutions and aid networks (the post-1945 'operating system'), not military strength; the Trump administration has done enormous, possibly permanent damage to that system in just over a year.

Volts · David Roberts 1 episode(s) · 29 insights

Volts ϟ 6.10.2026 – Colin McCormick + Doug Bryan (Why Is NERC So Worried About Data Centers?)74 min29 insights2026-06-11
▶ Watch on YouTube
NERC issued a historic Level 3 reliability alert because gigawatt-scale data center loads trip offline en masse in ~20ms during minor grid faults (e.g. ~1.5 GW of Virginia data centers dropped simultaneously in summer 2024), threatening cascading blackouts. The fix is mostly software/UPS ride-through and engineering at the load level rather than building gigawatt-scale ancillary markets; mandatory FERC-enforced standards are likely ~2027-2028. The panelists argue compliance is a minor lift versus supply-chain bottlenecks, that wilder data center demand projections are overheated, and that gas's 'firmness' edge over batteries is largely a mirage.
Why data center loads break grid reliability assumptions · 4The 2024 Virginia incident and tolerance mismatch · 4NERC's seven recommendations and the regulatory pipeline · 5Engineering fixes: software, batteries, inverters · 6Behind-the-meter, co-location, and the gas-vs-batteries debate · 5Demand projections, build-out pace, and the upside case · 5
Why data center loads break grid reliability assumptions 4
Data centers interface with the grid via active power electronics (UPS systems) that sense voltage/frequency and trip offline within roughly 20 milliseconds at the first sign of fluctuation, unlike conventional industrial loads with rotating machinery that ride through disturbances.
framework🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highNERCdata centersuninterruptible power suppliespower electronics
So what: The 'weird' fast-tripping behavior, not just scale, is the structural risk; investors should track UPS vendors and ride-through-capable hardware as a forced upgrade cycle.
≈ “That tripping offline happens in an incredibly fast, incredibly brief period of time — 20 milliseconds or something like that.” paraphrase
NERC's Level 3 alert is responding to gigawatt-plus-scale instantaneous load drops, not the 100 MW level that grids already handle, because clustered gigawatt data centers can compound to 2-3 GW dropping simultaneously beyond anything previously contemplated.
data_point🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highNERCdata centers
So what: Defines the magnitude of the reliability problem (GW not MW) that drives all downstream regulation and capex; relevant to siting and grid-asset demand.
≈ “Let's call it a gigawatt at least. That's what NERC are responding to with the Level 3 alert — gigawatt plus scale drops off the grid.” paraphrase
Correlated simultaneous disconnection happens without any cloud coordination because co-located data centers all sense the same physical grid conditions and are programmed identically, so they synchronize their trip-offs purely through electricity physics.
causal🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highdata centers
So what: Means the risk scales nonlinearly with geographic clustering; argues for dispersion policy and against data-center-alley concentration.
≈ “They're responding to the physics of electricity flow on the same physical grid.” paraphrase
AI training workloads create large GPU-driven load ramps up and down ('training bursts') whose ramp rates in some cases exceed the ramp-rate regulations that conventional generators are bound by.
data_point🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: mediumAI trainingGPUsdata centers
So what: Ramp-rate caps on loads are a coming constraint; flexible-compute scheduling and storage buffering become value-creating.
The 2024 Virginia incident and tolerance mismatch 4
In summer 2024 a minor transmission-line equipment failure in Virginia caused voltage dips so small humans wouldn't notice them, yet roughly 60 data centers (~1.5 GW) simultaneously dropped off the grid and switched to on-site generators.
data_point🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highVirginiadata centersPJMDominion
So what: Concrete proof the risk is realized not theoretical; the anchor event regulators and investors will keep referencing.
≈ “We're talking 60 data centers, I think it was around one and a half gigawatts, effectively simultaneously dropped off the grid in response to that single transmission fault.” paraphrase
The 2024 event forced PJM and Dominion to scale back power plant output to protect grid infrastructure and avoid cascading regional outages, because ~1.5 GW of inertia cannot be marshaled on a seconds timescale.
causal🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highPJMDominionVirginia
So what: Shows grid operators are already at the edge of manual mitigation; reinforces that the solution must be at the load level.
The core reliability problem is a tolerance mismatch: generators are required to ride through voltage and frequency disturbances while large data center loads trip at the first sign of trouble, creating severe instantaneous generation-load imbalances that regulators must now align.
framework🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highNERCgeneratorsdata centers
So what: Aligning load ride-through to generator standards is the central regulatory thrust; the equipment and standards bodies that enable it are the investable edge.
≈ “The large data centers basically trip off at the first sign of trouble. The generators ride through and I've got a pretty severe instantaneous imbalance between generation and load.” paraphrase
A NERC survey found roughly 87% of transmission owners and distribution providers lack clear facility design, modeling, and performance criteria for large loads in their interconnection requirements.
data_point🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highNERCtransmission ownersdistribution providers
So what: Quantifies the regulatory gap and the scale of new interconnection-study and modeling work coming; demand for grid-modeling and advisory services.
≈ “87% of transmission owners and distribution providers don't have clear facility design, modeling, and performance criteria for large loads in their interconnection requirements.” paraphrase
NERC's seven recommendations and the regulatory pipeline 5
NERC's seven essential actions bucket into communication/modeling (standardized data collection from computational loads, grid stability studies, commissioning, fault recording) and operational implementation (protection/ride-through gap fixes), applying to both new and existing computational loads.
framework🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highNERCISOsTSOsERCOT
So what: Maps the full compliance workload; retroactive applicability to existing loads is broader than ERCOT's new-only proposal and a bigger burden.
NERC's commissioning recommendation pushes for full-facility load and no-load tests with all compute equipment in place, but because data centers are built in phases this likely forces grid operators to continuously re-commission as load is added.
causal🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: mediumNERCdata centersgrid operators
So what: Phased-build re-commissioning is a recurring operational drag and a fuzzy trigger ('what counts as a change'); a friction point for developers.
NERC deliberately did not mandate voltage ride-through for large loads, instead using gentle 'where possible' language because no central IEEE-type standard yet exists and the industry needs validated, qualified compliant equipment first.
thesis🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highNERCIEEEdata centers
So what: Signals a phased path to mandates; watch IEEE/standards bodies and UPS qualification as the gating items before enforcement.
≈ “It's a lot more gentle — coordination with computational load customers where possible to maximize the ability to ride through.” paraphrase
Current NERC guidance is voluntary; grid entities must respond by August 2026, new reliability standards will then be drafted, and enforceable mandatory standards (via the NERC-to-FERC approval pipeline) realistically won't be in force until ~2027-2028.
prediction🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: mediumNERCFERC
So what: Gives a concrete timeline for when compliance capex and penalties bite; near-term build-out is largely unconstrained by these rules.
≈ “I'll put myself on the spot — but 2027, 2028 is probably a realistic time frame.” paraphrase
Only a scattered few grid regions have already implemented voltage ride-through requirements for large loads (Southwest Power Pool, Germany, FinGrid), and they use inconsistent ride-through and reconnect rules rather than a consolidated standard.
data_point🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: mediumSouthwest Power PoolGermanyFinGrid
So what: Fragmentation creates a standardization opportunity and regulatory arbitrage across regions for developers.
Engineering fixes: software, batteries, inverters 6
Germany's decade-ago '50.2 Hz problem'—where uniformly programmed rooftop solar inverters all tripped at 50.2 Hz creating an unintentional synchronized cascade risk—was solved cheaply by a software push that randomized each inverter's trip threshold.
anecdote🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highGermanysolar inverters
So what: Precedent that the data center trip problem is largely a software-randomization fix, not a costly hardware build—bearish on expensive ancillary buildouts.
≈ “There was a solution — to reprogram those inverters to have a little bit of randomization on the exact number, so they weren't all perfectly synchronized.” paraphrase
Many data center UPS units use 'event counters' (e.g. trip after 3-4 voltage events in a few seconds) governed by the conservative ITIC curve, and the easiest grid-citizen fix is a software change to remove or loosen that counter logic.
framework🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highUPSITIC curvedata centers
So what: Identifies low-cost software remediation as the first lever; hardware/battery upgrades are the second, more capital-intensive step.
Building grid-forming inverters with synthetic inertia is the better long-term reliability solution than building more spinning mass, since more inverter-based resources are coming online anyway and the fix is largely software.
thesis🗣 David Robertsconviction: mediumgrid-forming inverterssynthetic inertiabatteriessolarwind
So what: Favors grid-forming inverter and battery vendors over synchronous condenser/gas inertia plays over the long run.
Building a gigawatt-scale, seconds-response ancillary services market to absorb data center trips is the wrong, unscalable and uneconomic direction; the problem will mostly be solved at the computational-load level via ride-through engineering.
contrarian🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highancillary servicesbatteriesflywheelsdata centers
So what: Tempers the bull case for batteries-as-ancillary-revenue at GW scale for this specific use; value accrues to in-facility UPS/ride-through instead.
≈ “Trying to build a gigawatt scale ancillary services market to protect against this possible load trip issue is probably the wrong direction.” paraphrase
If GW-scale data centers must firm up power with batteries anyway, an exploding ancillary services market (growing in volume and price) could restore the value-stacking thesis for batteries that critics said was capped by quick ancillary-market saturation.
thesis🗣 David Robertsconviction: mediumbatteriesancillary services
So what: A nuanced bull case for grid-scale storage economics if ancillary demand truly scales up with data centers.
The reconnection problem mirrors the trip problem: a GW of UPS-backed load coming back online simultaneously is as dangerous as it dropping, and there are no broadly accepted standards yet for smooth, ramp-rate-limited reconnection.
framework🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highUPSdata centersgrid operators
So what: An under-discussed second-order risk and a future standards/equipment requirement; max-ramp-rate UPS logic is a likely mandate.
Behind-the-meter, co-location, and the gas-vs-batteries debate 5
Hybrid behind-the-meter setups (on-site generation plus a grid tie) can actually worsen grid stability complexity even as they reduce grid-tie draw, because there are more active components to balance; only a fully islanded data center eliminates the problem, and essentially nobody will do that.
causal🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: mediumbehind-the-meter generationdata centers
So what: Behind-the-meter is not a clean regulatory escape hatch; NERC wants visibility into the full campus including co-located generation.
NERC distinguishes co-location (data center physically sited at a power plant but both still connected to transmission, relevant to FERC's PJM co-location tariff directive) from behind-the-meter (generation electrically tied directly to load, not flowing through transmission).
framework🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highNERCFERCPJMco-location
So what: Clarifies the regulatory taxonomy investors must track; PJM co-location tariffs are a distinct live battleground.
In ERCOT, gas entries into the interconnection queue are up 150% while some renewables are withdrawing, as hyperscalers lunge for gas as a perceived firm-power and reliability solution.
data_point🗣 David Robertsconviction: highERCOTnatural gasrenewableshyperscalers
So what: Concrete near-term bullish signal for gas turbine demand in Texas; a headwind to the renewables decarb narrative.
≈ “In ERCOT, the number of gas entries into the interconnection queue is up 150%.” paraphrase
Projected new gas production for data centers runs around 10-11 BCF/day, but delivering it requires gas transmission, pipeline and distribution infrastructure that largely doesn't exist, raising the prospect of 'zombie' or partially completed gas projects whose economics fail one-to-three years in.
prediction🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: mediumnatural gaspipelinesmethane
So what: Cautions against pricing in full gas buildout; pipeline/permitting bottlenecks and methane leakage are the swing factors.
≈ “10 or 11 BCF a day is what you're seeing. That's a lot.” paraphrase
Gas's vaunted 'firmness' over batteries is largely a mirage and partly cultural ('masculine energy'); operationally both deliver firm electrons, and long-duration storage advances may let batteries provide the firm power hyperscalers seek.
contrarian🗣 David Robertsconviction: mediumnatural gasbatterieslong-duration energy storageForm Energy
So what: Bullish long-duration storage thesis (Form Energy named); challenges the gas-procurement consensus as duration/cycling limits get solved.
≈ “It's hard not to see an element of masculine energy involved in preferring gas or nuclear for your firmness over batteries.” paraphrase
Demand projections, build-out pace, and the upside case 5
Carbon Direct's analysis holds that the wilder data center demand projections are overheated and a majority of data centers currently in the interconnection queue will probably not get built.
thesis🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: highCarbon Directdata centersinterconnection queue
So what: Direct fade of the most aggressive AI-power-demand forecasts; even a ~40% completion rate is historically enormous but well below headline numbers.
≈ “A majority of the data centers in the queue are probably not going to get built.” paraphrase
Contrary to the assumption that NERC's requirements will materially slow data center build-out, the incremental compliance effort is minuscule versus supply-chain bottlenecks like securing switchgear, transformers and bulk power at all sites.
contrarian🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highNERCswitchgeartransformersdata centers
So what: Reframes the binding constraint as supply chain (switchgear/transformers/turbines), not reliability rules—where the real bottleneck investments and shortages lie.
≈ “I actually think the incremental effort here is pretty minuscule, especially compared to the other challenges that rapid data center buildout already faces.” paraphrase
Compliance only becomes a real headache if regulators mandate ride-through before a clear standard and broadly available compliant equipment exist; if compliant UPSs are on the market, the developer lift is minor.
causal🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: highNERCUPSdata centers
So what: The key risk to watch is regulatory timing getting ahead of equipment supply—a specific failure mode for project timelines.
Forecasting data center load is best triangulated from three sources—hyperscaler capex announcements and SEC filings, GPU/chip shipment data, and the interconnection queue (which requires a significant haircut).
framework🗣 Doug Bryanconviction: mediumhyperscalersGPUsinterconnection queueSEC filings
So what: A reusable methodology for sizing AI power demand; the interconnection-queue haircut is the critical analytical discipline.
A correctly designed gigawatt-scale data center—stacking on-site generation, battery backup, controllable flexible load and fast power electronics—could function as a giant virtual power plant and become a net positive contributor to grid stability via frequency regulation.
thesis🗣 Colin McCormickconviction: mediumdata centersvirtual power plantsbatteriesdemand response
So what: Upside scenario: GW data centers as grid assets/VPPs, a future revenue stream and a reframing of the load-vs-grid relationship.
“That's a decent recipe for being a grid asset.” ▶ 1:09:25

RealFi vs incentive-driven RWA 5
Pharos's founder argues most current RWA is unsustainable because it is looped risky assets wrapped across protocols to stack incentives, which amplifies risk rather than creating real value.

Neo-banks and regulatory tailwinds 2
Pharos's founder states a Clarity Act passed the day before recording, which he expects to pave the way for a crowded wave of US neo-banks by providing regulatory certainty.
prediction🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highClarity Actneo-banksUnited Statesregulation
So what: Regulatory clarity is framed as the unlock for US crypto neo-banks; expect a surge of new entrants competing for payment/programmable-money infra.
“I mean Clarity Act passed yesterday and then that basically paved the way for the neo banks particularly in US.” ▶ 16:47
Neo-banks are aggregators bundling payment rails with crypto, tokenized equities and programmable money, and that programmable money needs a place to issue, settle and circulate that Pharos aims to capture.
thesis🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: mediumneo-banksPharosstablecoinstokenized equitiesprogrammable money
So what: Settlement layer for neo-bank programmable money is the prize; whoever wins neo-bank integrations captures recurring transaction flow.
Adoption reality check and market timing 4
Pharos's founder argues the industry is overestimating institutional adoption, which he says is a real trend but 'not there yet,' partly because frequent on-chain security incidents deter risk-averse institutions.
contrarian🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highinstitutional adoptionDeFisecurity
So what: Tempers bullish institutional-adoption theses; positions investors to expect a 6-12+ month lag rather than imminent trillion-dollar inflows.
“There's a people that believe there's a lot of institutional adoptions that happening like right now ... I totally see the trend, but it's not there yet.” ▶ 30:42
On-chain security incidents are increasing because attackers are using AI agents trained to find bugs 24/7, which the founder says will further delay institutional adoption if unaddressed.
causal🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: mediumsecurityAI agentsDeFi
So what: AI-driven exploit discovery is a rising systemic risk; security/audit infra and AI-defense tooling become more valuable as exploit cadence rises.
“I think some of them are filled by some AI capabilities now because people actually train the AI agent to be able to find the bugs like 24/7.” ▶ 31:05
The founder calls expectations of a trillion-dollar institutional inflow within 3-6 months an overestimation, arguing it more likely requires 6-12 months or longer to properly resolve security and use-case issues.
prediction🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: mediuminstitutional adoptionsecurity
So what: Sets a concrete timeline expectation for institutional capital; near-term inflow disappointment is a risk for adoption-driven valuations.
“We'll see maybe like a trillion dollar uh of of growth in the next three months, six months. I think that's overestimation.” ▶ 32:03
The founder argues the industry is underestimating remaining-99% interest and that current attention shift to AI (with people leaving crypto) makes this the ideal time for serious long-term builders, likely fueling a bull market in a year or two.
contrarian🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highAIcryptobuilders
So what: Classic build-in-the-bear thesis; talent/attention rotation to AI may be a contrarian buy signal for durable crypto infra projects on a 1-2 year horizon.
“Maybe some people will see that right now is a better market but not necessarily for builders ... now is the perfect time to build.” ▶ 32:55
Success definition and target market 4
Pharos's North Star metric is the number of real use cases addressing genuine business problems, deliberately downweighting common KPIs like TVL, active users and trading volume that can be gamed by incentives.
framework🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highPharosTVL
So what: Signals that headline TVL for this chain should be discounted as a vanity metric; evaluate real cash-flow use cases instead.
“my number one like uh key I guess the uh uh measurement is that how many real use cases we have been building.”
The founder defines ultimate success as mass adoption of Web2 capital and Web2 users, noting on-chain finance is still under 1% of the overall financial world with room to grow to 5-10% and beyond.
thesis🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highWeb2 capitalon-chain financePharos
So what: Frames the TAM as the <1% on-chain share of global finance; the investable thesis is the on-ramp of off-chain capital, not rotating existing crypto liquidity.
“On chain capital right now today ... if you compare it with the overall financial world, we're still less than 1%.” ▶ 29:10
Pharos targets unbanked Southeast Asian populations who lack bank accounts but rely heavily on apps, requiring a flexible payment rail that serves both institutional (low-fee, heavy KYC/KYB) and retail (friendly, high-fee) on/off ramps cost-efficiently.
thesis🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highPharosSoutheast Asiaunbankedpayment railsKYCKYB
So what: Emerging-market unbanked retail is the demand-side bet; affordable on/off-ramp infra for these users is the make-or-break execution risk.
“It doesn't really serve, uh for example, unbanked population in Southeast Asia ... they typically don't don't have a bank account, but they heavily relying on their app” ▶ 3:04
Pharos's founder calls inclusive finance a niche but blue-ocean market he expects to grow significantly over the next 12 months, 3 years and decade.
prediction🗣 David (Pharos founder)conviction: highPharosinclusive finance
So what: Multi-horizon growth claim for the inclusive-finance niche; a thesis to revisit against actual adoption milestones at 12 months and 3 years.
“This is this is a niche market but at the same time this is also a blue ocean market.” ▶ 28:10
